Guillaume,

I have one question/remark on your comments:

You say:
 -: No, you cannot use semaphores with pthreads. Blocking on a semaphore
will block the whole process - no just the current thread.

However, reading the Solaris/Linux manpages I come to a different conclusion
(see extract of man pages below message).

The conclusion I draw is that I can use semaphores to synchronize threads.
What is your opinion on this? Furthermore, Solaris supports the concept of
Light Weight Processes and on current Linux systems a thread is actually
implemented as a separate process with it's own pid. I also wrote a simple
test program (I modified syn2.c and ran it on Linux) that uses threads and
semaphores for synchronization and it works fine!

Regards,
Sander van Loo


>From man semaphore:
...If  the  semaphore  count
drops  to zero, which means no available resources,  threads
attempting to decrement the semaphore will block  until  the
count is greater than zero.

Semaphores can synchronize threads in this process and other
processes  if  they  are  allocated  in writable memory  and
shared among the cooperating processes  (see  mmap(2)),  and
have been initialized for this purpose.
...

>From man sem_init:
...
If the pshared argument  is  zero,  then  the  semaphore  is
shared  between  threads  of the process; any thread in this
process  can   use   sem   for   performing   sem_wait(3RT),
sem_trywait(3RT), sem_post(3RT), and sem_destroy(3RT) opera-
tions. The use of the semaphore by threads other than  those
created in the same process is undefined.
...

>From Linux man sem_init:
...
Semaphores are counters for resources shared between threads. The basic
operations on semaphores are: increment the counter atomically, and wait
until the counter is non-null and decrement it atomically.
...

>  You say:
>   -: No, you cannot use semaphores with pthreads. Blocking on a
>  semaphore will block the whole process - no just the current
>  thread. 
>  
>  However, reading the Solaris/Linux manpages I come to a different
>  conclusion (see extract of man pages below message).
>  
>  The conclusion I draw is that I can use semaphores to synchronize
>  threads.  What is your opinion on this? Furthermore, Solaris
>  supports the concept of Light Weight Processes and on current Linux
>  systems a thread is actually implemented as a separate process with
>  it's own pid. I also wrote a simple test program (I modified syn2.c
>  and ran it on Linux) that uses threads and semaphores for
>  synchronization and it works fine!

You are right, I just tried and it worked fine. However, the SysV
specification is not extremely precise on the subject (threads
vs. processes). So I wouldn't bet it will work on any Unix system.

Solaris lightweight processes are just not processes, but
threads. Lightweight process is the old name for a thread. The only
originality of Solaris to that respect is that, since it implemented
its LWP before the Posix specification was out, it now supports the
two sets of interfaces to the same mechanisms.

Linux indeed doesn't distinguish between threads and processes, but as
far as I know it is the only system to do so.

Anyway, you will have noticed that I didn't remove points on that
question :-)




                                                 G.

Geen bug, maar een 'feature'. De ads die je wilt sturen mogen geen dirs
bevatten (dus moeten in de workdir van adsend staan). Probleem is dat de
adserver geen subdirs gaat aanmaken enzo, dus dan zou ie eerst het pad er af
moeten strippen. De adserver slaat deze ads vervolgens op in de subdir ads.

Guillaume,

We have found a small bug. The adserver only accepts ads that do not contain
a directory in the filename, therefore adsend can only send ads that are in
its current working directory. I.e. 'adsend <budget> <src acct> <dst acct>
ad1.gif ad2.jpg' will work fine, but things like 'adsend <budget> <src acct>
<dst acct> /home/sander/ad1.gif /home/erik/ad2.gif' will not work. 

Regards,
Sander van Loo
Erik van Zijst
